Another Statement by the Chinese Representative to Japan (II)

The Spirit of the Treaty of Portsmouth 81

The Chinese government's views on the economic severance of relations with Japan have been explained in detail in the speech of the Chinese representative to the Council and in the memorandum submitted by the Chinese representative to the League of Nations, so there is no need to repeat them here. However, if the economic severance of relations is, as the Japanese representative claimed, a disguised form of war, then how should the Japanese army's invasion of Manchuria, accompanied by artillery, airplanes, machine gun teams, and all other weapons, to kill Chinese people and damage Chinese property, be described? In short, the initial cause of China's current economic severance of relations with Japan was the massacre of Chinese people in the Korean region in July 1931, with the tacit approval of the Japanese police authorities. As a result, 142 Chinese people died, 546 were injured, 91 were missing, and property losses exceeded hundreds of thousands of Japanese yen. After the September 18th Incident occurred and the Japanese army invaded Manchuria, this economic severance movement became even more active. Every past economic severance movement against Japan has been a means of resistance against Japanese military aggression or provocation, out of extreme helplessness. This is an obvious fact. In the past 30 years, there have been nine economic severances against Japan. However, the Japanese representative only pointed out four times, believing that they could not be regarded as actions to resist Japanese military actions. This is tantamount to tacitly admitting that the other five times can indeed be regarded as actions to resist Japanese military aggression. If we examine the four times pointed out by the Japanese side one by one, the economic severance in 1908 was the result of Japan issuing an ultimatum after the Erh-Chen-Wan incident in order to satisfy its arbitrary demands. The economic severance in 1909 was the result of Japan making similar provocative demands during the Sino-Japanese negotiations on the reconstruction of the Andong-Poyang Railway. The economic severance in 1919 was the result of the Japanese refusing to withdraw their troops from eastern China under the pretext of eliminating German influence, which aroused natural resistance from the Chinese people. The economic severance in 1923 was an expression of the Chinese people's resistance to the Japanese government's insistence on implementing the 1915 agreement based on the so-called Twenty-One Demands.

The Japanese representative cited many of the British government's notes to the Soviet representative regarding the so-called Zinoviev letter incident, but in fact, it has nothing to do with the issue of economic severance. The organization of the Chinese National Government and the Moscow Soviet Government are completely different. Moreover, considering the nature and extent of the Japanese army's aggressive actions in Manchuria and even Shanghai, Tianjin, and other places, the adoption and maintenance of an economic severance movement by the Chinese people or groups as a legitimate act of self-defense is fully justified.

In the Japanese representative's statement, he mentioned the Shanghai Incident from January to March 1932. Regarding this matter, the Chinese representative has submitted several documents to the Lytton Commission for reference. These documents clearly stated that on the evening of January 28, Japanese armed forces, assisted by machine guns, first attacked Chinese police and then attacked Chinese troops. However, the Chinese police and troops took a strict defensive posture. Therefore, the Japanese claim that Japanese troops were fired upon is inconsistent with the facts.

Furthermore, the method of dividing the defense of the Shanghai International Settlement was jointly agreed upon by the military authorities of various countries. The Chinese authorities did not participate, were not notified, and had no reason to be bound by it. Moreover, the method drafted by the commanders of the various countries' land and naval forces did not allow the Japanese army to invade Chinese areas outside the International Settlement or attack Chinese troops stationed near it.

According to authoritative reports from neutral parties such as the Consular Committee appointed by the League of Nations, the Shanghai Incident was entirely due to Japan's premeditated plan, the purpose of which was to intimidate the Chinese people and reduce their spirit of resistance to the situation in Manchuria. The result of the Shanghai Incident was the death of more than 24,000 Chinese people, property damage of 150 million yuan, and the suffering of Shanghai residents was incalculable.

Even more strangely, the Japanese representative asked the Council to pay attention to the greater loss of life of the Chinese people caused by the Chinese army when the Japanese army attacked Shanghai and caused the loss of Chinese lives. This is the most obvious example of the peculiar psychology of the Japanese government. These two things are absolutely unrelated. It is incomprehensible why the Japanese representative confuses these two things. Does Japan mean that the internal strife that is unavoidable during China's period of great political transition, much of which was instigated by Japan, can be a reason for the Japanese army to use force to invade Chinese territory and cause more pain to the Chinese people? This kind of attitude is obviously contrary to the righteousness and conscience of all mankind.

(To be continued)

Zhu and Mao's Bandit Troops Attack Nanfeng Again and Collapse

Mao Bingwen Fights Bloodily for Four Days and Nights, Piles of Bandit Corpses Like Mountains 82

Nanchang Correspondence, Zhu and Mao's pseudo-First Army Corps Lin Biao, pseudo-Third Army Corps Peng Dehuai, pseudo-Fifth Army Corps Dong Zhentang, pseudo-Twenty-Second Army Luo Binghui, and the recently joined Fang Shao pseudo-Tenth Army from Guixi and Peng's bandits, after withdrawing from Jinxi and Guixi, invaded the northwest of Fujian Province through Zixi and Lichuan. The pseudo-army hospitals, wounded bandits, and various rear pseudo-agencies moved to the Jianning and Ninghua areas. However, the bandits' whereabouts are elusive and unpredictable. According to investigations, at the beginning of this month, the bandits in the Zixi, Guangze, and Jianning areas traveled at night and hid during the day, and gathered again in Lichuan County. Since the 10th, Mao and Peng suddenly personally led the main forces of each unit to besiege Nanfeng in several ways. The scattered bandits lurking in various places in the Huangshidu area at the junction of Jinxi and Zixi also attacked northward at the same time to respond. According to military reports from the front line, the bandits attacked Nanfeng. (A) Peng's bandits commanded the pseudo-Third Army Corps to attack Xinfeng Street from Xiaoshi at the junction of Nancheng, Lichuan, and Guangze, hoping to cut off the connection between the national army stationed in Nancheng and Nanfeng, in order to contain the division at Taliwei and bypass the north of Nancheng. (B) Fang Shao's pseudo-Tenth Army, 82nd and 83rd Regiments, etc., attacked the east of Nanfeng from Shixia below Shangguan. (C) The pseudo-Fifth Army Corps took Zhongxian Town, Lichuan as its base and attacked the south of Nanfeng. (D) Luo Binghui led the pseudo-Twenty-Second Army to advance to Shanshi, more than ten miles away from Nanfeng City. (E) Another unit came down from Guangchang and crossed the Fenglin Ferry to the west of Nanfeng. The above units adopted a four-sided encirclement strategy and attacked the county seat fiercely. The garrison troops Mao Bingwen and others withdrew into the city and defended it desperately. The bandits repeatedly gathered their teams to charge, and Mao's troops waited until the bandits approached before bombarding them with machine guns and artillery. The bandits also fought bravely, one after another, and the sound of guns and cannons was endless from the 10th to the 14th. The bandit corpses piled up like mountains. According to military intelligence, the bandits had suffered too many casualties and exhausted their elite troops, and were now retreating in their original directions. The bandits who committed crimes in Nancheng, Yihuang, and Huangshidu also dispersed one after another, since the purpose of covering had been lost. There were no major changes in other places. However, the Guangdong and Guangxi troops have already set out to encircle and suppress the Jiangxi bandits, and will soon join forces with the Fujian army to advance towards Yudu, Huichang, and Xingguo, hoping to defeat Zhu and Mao's main forces in two months, eliminate the remaining bandits in one month, and complete the task of suppressing the bandits in three months as desired. (February 11th)

Jiangxi Province Organizes Iron Shoulder Teams

The Position of Commander-in-Chief is Concurrently Held by Xiong Shihui, with the Task of Assisting in the Bandit Suppression Work 83

Nanchang Correspondence, The Jiangxi Provincial Party and Government Conference held its third regular meeting at 9:00 AM on the 15th in the Provincial Government Conference Hall. Attendees included Provincial Government Chairman Xiong Shihui, Provincial Reorganization Committee Wang Guanying, Liu Jiashu, Li Zhongxiang, Fan Zhengbo, Provincial Government Members Xiong Yuxi, Yuan Liang, Gong Xuesui, Xiong Sui, Li Dezhao, Wu Jiantao, and Secretary-General Liu Tigan was present as an observer. Chairman Xiong Shihui. It was learned that the special item discussed on that day was the organizational regulations of the Iron Shoulder Team, which had been amended and passed. The number of team members was determined to be 5,640, divided into 47 teams, with 120 members per team, and it was stipulated that strong men should be selected from various counties to organize the teams. In addition to Linchuan County organizing three teams, Nanchang, Fengcheng, Qingjiang, Xingan, Gaoan, Fengxin, Jiujiang, Pengze, Fuliang, Poyang, Wuning, and Dean counties each organized two teams. Xinjian, Jinxian, Anyi, Shanggao, Yifeng, Yichun, Fenyi, Ruichang, Yongxiu, Hukou, Xingzi, Jingan, Yugan, Guixi, Yujiang, Yihuang, Ji'an, Jishui, Taihe, Dongxiang, and Xinyu counties each organized one team, and it was required that they be fully organized and established within this month, so that they could be sent to the front to undertake transportation and other work. The position of Commander-in-Chief was concurrently held by Xiong himself, and the captains of each team were concurrently held by the county magistrates of each county. One vice-captain was selected by the county magistrates to serve as an officer. Each member of the Iron Shoulder Team received a monthly salary of twelve yuan. If they also pushed a cart, they would receive an additional one yuan per day for transportation. If the cart was damaged, seven yuan would be compensated for each cart.

Turmoil in the Hunan Provincial Party Committee

Tan Changkai Ordered to Return, He Jian Strongly Resigns from the Provincial Party Committee 84

Changsha Correspondence, Hunan's party affairs have always been divided into the "small organization" and "anti-small organization" factions. The small organization focuses on the lower-level party departments in various counties, while the anti-small organization relies on the power of the provincial government to operate. The infighting and secret struggles are extremely fierce. Major disturbances such as the demolition of the Changsha Municipal Party Department and the Hunan Zhongshan Daily, which was run by the Provincial Party Department, have been staged. In the past two years, although there has been superficial peace, in fact they have still failed to cooperate. Last year, the Third Provincial Congress of Hunan was held, using the Category B election method to elect double the number of executive and supervisory committee members of the Provincial Party Department. He Jian, Tan Changkai, Huang Jiasheng, Wang Qi, Zhang Jiong, Xie Zuyou, and Zhu Haohuai were presented to the Central Committee for approval as members of the Executive Committee, and Peng Guojun, Xiao Fengwei, Peng Yunbin, Meng Qingxuan, and Liu Baoshu were presented as members of the Supervisory Committee, establishing a formal Provincial Party Department. Of the seven members of the Executive Committee, Wang, Zhang, and Xie belonged to the small organization, He, Tan, and Huang belonged to the anti-small organization, and Zhu Haohuai was from Guangdong and belonged to the neutral faction. All five members of the Supervisory Committee belonged to the small organization, which shows that the real power of Hunan's party affairs is in the hands of the small organization faction. Last year, Yao Xuehuai, a key member of the small organization faction and a member of the Executive Committee of the Changsha Municipal Party Department, criticized the government's military expenditure and fled the province, causing a party-government dispute. Wang Qi and Zhang Jiong then left Hunan for Nanjing, never to return. The rift between the two factions within the Party Department has become increasingly apparent since then, but it has not yet erupted. On the 2nd of this month, the 56th Standing Committee Meeting of the Central Party Department resolved that Tan Changkai, a member of the Hunan Provincial Party Department, be transferred back, and his vacancy be filled by Xiao Jun, the first alternate member. It was discovered that Xiao was a member of the small organization faction, and He Jian and others were very dissatisfied, believing that this matter must have been done by Wang Qi and Zhang Jiong in Nanjing. The National Daily, which is run by the Provincial Government, even published an article criticizing this move by the Central Committee as illegal. At the same time, the Changsha Municipal Party Department also submitted a request to the Central Party Department to withdraw the transfer order, and telegraphed various county and municipal party departments, asking them to adopt the same position. Party departments in counties such as Yongxing, Yizhang, and Hengshan responded by sending telegrams in support. He Jian also telegraphed the Central Committee to resign from his position as a member of the Executive Committee of the Provincial Party Department, stating that he would no longer be responsible for party affairs. He also stopped attending meetings of the Provincial Party Department from the 8th of this month. The recent standing committees of the Provincial Party Department have consequently lacked a quorum and have been suspended three times, and party affairs have come to a standstill. Li Faquan, the secretary of the Provincial Party Department who was advancing and retreating with He Jian, also submitted his resignation on the 15th and did not go to the office to work. The Hunan party turmoil is already looming. (February 16th)

Songjiang

Part 2: Progress of the Sino-Japanese Dispute in the League of Nations 85

(1) A Brief Account of the Development of the Incident: Since the submission of this case to the League of Nations, the resolutions of the Council and the Assembly have repeatedly depended on the changes in the situation in the Far East. At the beginning of the Sino-Japanese dispute, when the Chinese government referred the case to the League of Nations under Article 11 of the Covenant, the scope of the incident only extended to Shenyang and some other locations in the three northeastern provinces. At that time, the Council repeatedly received assurances from Japan that Japan had no territorial ambitions in Manchuria and that as long as the lives and property of Japanese residents were guaranteed, Japan could withdraw its troops to within the South Manchuria Railway zone. This was the gist of the resolutions of September 30, 1932, and the draft resolution of October 24, the latter of which was agreed to by the entire Council except for Japan, allowing the Council to seek further commitment from the Japanese delegation. After the Japanese representative rejected the aforementioned draft resolution, Japan insisted that fundamental problems between China and Japan must be resolved, prompting the Council to propose further measures within the scope of ensuring the implementation of the September 30 resolution, in order to achieve a final and fundamental resolution of the problems between the two countries. On December 10, 1932, the Council accepted Japan's proposal to organize a "Commission of Inquiry" to investigate the situation on the ground and to report to the Council on "any circumstances affecting international relations that may disrupt the peace between China and Japan or the understanding on which peace is maintained." Between December and March, the situation in the Far East deteriorated significantly. Japanese troops completely occupied South Manchuria and began to invade North Manchuria. Outside Manchuria, fierce conflicts between formal Chinese and Japanese troops had begun in Shanghai and were ongoing. At the same time, in the areas occupied by Japanese troops, administrative organs were reorganized, forming an independent state called "Manchukuo," denying China's sovereignty. Subsequently, China requested the Council to handle this dispute not only under Article 11 of the Covenant but also under Articles 10 and 15. On February 19, 1932, as a result of China's request under Article 15, paragraph 9, the Council submitted the dispute to the Assembly. The report of the Commission of Inquiry was necessary for a detailed examination of the substance of the dispute. Therefore, from January onwards, before receiving the report of the Commission of Inquiry, the main tasks of the Council and later the Assembly were to do their best to cease hostilities, prevent further expansion of the situation, and maintain the rights of the parties and the principles of the Covenant, so as not to be adversely affected by any "fait accompli," and to clearly express the League's attitude towards the dispute with the resolution of March 11, declaring that member states of the League should not recognize any situation, treaty, or agreement created in violation of the Covenant or the Pact of Paris until the dispute is resolved in accordance with the Covenant. Hostilities in Shanghai ended, but in the three northeastern provinces, Japanese troops or "Manchukuo" government troops continued to fight against informal Chinese troops. In September 1932, a few days after the report of the Commission of Inquiry was signed in Peiping, the Japanese government's attitude underwent a fundamental change, namely, the Japanese government recognized the government of "Manchukuo." The report of the Commission of Inquiry could not reach Geneva before the end of September, that is, before the expiration of the six-month period, which is the deadline stipulated in the Covenant for sending the report to the Assembly in accordance with Article 15. Therefore, the Assembly, with the consent of the parties, decided on July 1 to extend the necessary deadline, but understood that this extension should not be regarded as a precedent. The Commission of Inquiry was thus able to complete its report locally, and the parties were able to submit their opinions on the report, while the Council and the Assembly were able to examine the materials thus obtained. The examination of these materials and the exchange of views with the parties continued from November 1932 to early February 1933. After discussions in the Council, the Assembly, based on the materials and conclusions contained in the report of the Commission of Inquiry, attempted to resolve the dispute through negotiations between the parties in accordance with Article 15, paragraph 3, but without effect, leading the Assembly to adopt this report in accordance with paragraph 4 of the same article.

(2) The Reasons for Submitting the Dispute to the League of Nations: The events that occurred in South Manchuria from September 18 to 19, 1931, and the initial discussions of the Council on China's request. The Council, due to the actions taken by the Japanese army in Manchuria on the night of September 18, 1931, because an incident occurred near Shenyang, in the South Manchuria Railway zone protected by the Japanese army, the Japanese military authorities, citing the necessity of military precautions, sent troops outside the zone, especially to Chinese cities adjacent to the zone and to the railway lines terminating in Shenyang. Chinese cities such as Shenyang, Changchun, Andong, Yingkou, and others were occupied, and Chinese troops were dispersed or disarmed. On September 21, China, in accordance with Article 11 of the Covenant, requested the Council to take immediate steps to prevent further changes in the situation that would endanger international peace, to restore the situation before the incident, and to determine the nature and amount of compensation due to the Republic of China. On September 22, the Council authorized the President of the Council (i.e., the Spanish representative Lerroux) to send an urgent request to the two governments to prevent any action that would escalate the situation or hinder the peaceful resolution of the issue, and to advise the two governments to immediately proceed with the withdrawal of their troops without endangering the lives and property of their people. On September 28, the President of the Council, based on the reports received from the two parties, explained the situation to the General Assembly, stating that "the withdrawal of Japanese troops to within the South Manchuria Railway zone is underway," and that "on September 28, the Japanese representative had declared in the Council that the withdrawal of troops was proceeding, except for Shenyang and Jilin, where only a small number of Japanese troops were stationed outside the railway zone in Xinmin and Zhengjiatun to protect Japanese residents from attacks by Chinese soldiers and bandits, as these soldiers and bandits were disturbing the aforementioned areas." The situation was such when the Council adopted the following resolution on September 30 (see page 2307 of the League of Nations Official Journal, December 1931). The resolution was as follows: (1) The Council takes note of the replies of the Chinese and Japanese governments to the urgent appeal of the President of the Council and the steps taken to respond to this appeal. (2) The Council considers the statement of the Japanese government that it has no territorial ambitions in the eastern provinces to be important. (3) The Council takes note of the statement of the Japanese representative that the Japanese army has begun to withdraw, and that the Japanese government will continue to withdraw its troops to the railway zone as quickly as possible, in proportion to the safety of the lives and property of Japanese residents being effectively guaranteed, and hopes to fully implement this intention as soon as possible. (4) The Council takes note of the statement of the Chinese representative that the Chinese government will be responsible for the safety of the lives and property of Japanese residents outside the area when the Japanese army continues to withdraw and the local Chinese officials and police are restored. (5) The Council is convinced that both governments are extremely keen to avoid taking any action that would disrupt the peace and understanding between the two countries and is aware that the Chinese and Japanese representatives have assured that their respective governments will take all necessary steps to prevent the expansion of the scope of the incident or the further aggravation of the situation. (6) The Council requests the two parties to do their utmost to restore normal relations between the two countries as soon as possible and to continue and promptly complete the implementation of the aforementioned guarantees in order to achieve this goal. (7) The Council requests the two parties to fully provide the Council with information on the development of the situation at any time. (8) The Council decides that, unless an unexpected event occurs that requires an immediate meeting, it will reconvene in Geneva on October 10 to consider the situation at that time. (9) The Council authorizes its President, after consulting with his colleagues, especially the representatives of the two countries concerned, to cancel the meeting of the Council on October 14 if he considers that, based on the information on the progress of the situation obtained from the parties concerned or from other members, there is no need to reconvene the meeting. (The Council's wishes were not realized.) On October 10, the Chinese delegation, due to the continued active offensive by the Japanese army and the bombing of Jinzhou, the seat of the temporary provincial government, by aircraft, requested the Council to convene an emergency meeting. When the Council met in September, it decided to send the minutes of the Council and documents relating to the Sino-Japanese dispute to the U.S. government. At the same time, the U.S. government also expressed its full sympathy with the League of Nations' attitude. On October 16, the Council decided to continue to cooperate with the U.S. government and invited the U.S. government to send a representative to attend the Council in order to discuss the relationship between the provisions of the Pact of Paris and the unfortunate current situation in Manchuria and to observe the situation. The U.S. government sent the same note to the Chinese and Japanese governments regarding all other discussions of the Council on the issue. On October 22, the President of the Council (the French representative Briand) submitted a draft resolution, which was unanimously agreed to except for the parties concerned. The draft resolution, after stating the commitments undertaken by the Chinese and Japanese governments in accordance with the resolution of September 30 of the previous month and the declaration of the Japanese representative that Japan had no territorial intentions in Manchuria, requested the Japanese government to immediately begin withdrawing the Japanese army to within the railway zone and to withdraw all troops by the next meeting, and requested the Chinese government to prepare measures to receive the areas from which the Japanese army withdrew to ensure the safety of the lives and property of Japanese residents. The draft resolution, for the purpose of implementation, also slightly specified the detailed measures. The draft resolution also suggested to the Chinese and Japanese governments that, after the withdrawal of the Japanese army, the two countries should immediately begin direct negotiations to resolve all outstanding issues between China and Japan, especially regarding the recent incidents and disputes arising from the situation of the Eastern Provinces Railway. To achieve the above objectives, the Council suggested that the two parties should organize a conciliation committee or a similar permanent body. Finally, it was proposed that the Council should reconvene on November 16. On December 13, the Chinese representative accepted the draft resolution as a minimum. The Japanese representative then submitted a counter-proposal, stating that the Japanese government, in view of the tension and circumstances in Manchuria, could not predict a definite date for the withdrawal of the Japanese army. The Japanese government believed that restoring a calmer state of mind was absolutely necessary and therefore decided on several principles as the basis for the regular relations between China and Japan. However, the Japanese representative had no authority to include these principles in the resolution or to discuss them in detail at the Council meeting, as these principles should only be the basic conditions for direct negotiations between the two parties. The Council believed that, as it was unaware of the content of the "principles," it could not, of course, mention them in the draft resolution. Due to the opposition of the Japanese representative, the Council was not able to pass the resolution on October 24 and adjourned the meeting to November 18. After the meeting on October 24, the Chinese representative, on behalf of the Chinese government, made the following declaration to the President of the Council: China, like other member states of the League of Nations, is bound by the Covenant and conscientiously abides by all treaty obligations. The Chinese government is determined to fulfill all obligations stipulated in the Covenant. To demonstrate this will, it is extremely willing to resolve all disputes with Japan regarding the interpretation of treaties through arbitration or judicial means in accordance with the provisions of Article 13 of the Covenant. To implement this will, the Chinese government is willing to conclude an arbitration treaty with Japan, just as the United States and China recently concluded an arbitration treaty, or as most arbitration treaties concluded between member states of the League of Nations in recent years.

(3) Progress of Japanese Military Action in North Manchuria: After the Council met in October, the Japanese army again engaged in attacks near the Nenjiang Bridge of the Taonan Railway in Manchuria. The Nenjiang Bridge was destroyed in October by the troops of Ma Zhanshan, the Chairman of Heilongjiang Province, to prevent the advance of Zhang Haipeng's troops. According to the Chinese side, Zhang Haipeng was taking the offensive under the command of the Japanese army. To justify the interference in the repair of the Nenjiang Bridge as reasonable, the Japanese government unilaterally declared to the Chinese government that the transfer of the Taonan Railway was based on a contract. The South Manchuria Railway Company built the line, and the Chinese side had not yet repaid the debt and was unwilling to convert the debt into a loan. Therefore, the line could be considered to belong to the South Manchuria Railway Company, which was extremely concerned about protecting the company's property and maintaining the railway's transportation. On November 2, the Japanese government declared that, at the request of the South Manchuria and Taonan Railway Bureaus, it had dispatched a team of engineers on that day, protected by infantry, artillery, and air force, to repair the railway bridge. The Japanese army immediately clashed with the Chinese army, which refused to retreat, and repelled it. In mid-November, Japanese troops then advanced to and crossed the Chinese Eastern Railway and captured Angangxi, and subsequently captured Qiqihar on November 19.

(4) Measures to Reorganize the Civil Administration in Manchuria: While military action was progressing in Manchuria, the reorganization of the civil administration was also taking place simultaneously. In the case of Shenyang, after the disintegration of the political situation following the September 18 Incident, the local government was initially placed under the responsibility of Japanese Colonel Doihara. Subsequently, on October 20, Zhao Xinbo, a Chinese national who graduated from Tokyo Imperial University with a doctorate in law, was appointed as the mayor. At that time, the former Liaoning Provincial Government had moved to Jinzhou, and another Liaoning Provincial Government was organized to counter it. The Local Maintenance Committee, organized on September 24, was reorganized into the Liaoning Provincial Autonomous Public Office in October. On November 7, the Autonomous Public Office was reorganized into the Acting Liaoning Provincial Government, declaring its separation from the former Northeast Government and the Nanjing National Government. At the same time, a Supreme Guidance Department was established, part of whose functions were to guide and supervise the provincial government and encourage local autonomy. All these new organs, as well as the banks issuing banknotes, were assigned Japanese advisors, most of whom were influential officials of the South Manchuria Railway. The Chinese representative insisted that the establishment and maintenance of all new organs in Shenyang, Jilin, and other locations occupied by the Japanese army should be the responsibility of the Japanese army, as these organs were all puppets and products of the Japanese army. The Japanese representative replied that the Japanese authorities had no other way but to encourage the Chinese to organize their own groups to maintain order. If these groups could fulfill their responsibilities, it would make it easier to realize the wishes repeatedly and formally expressed by the Japanese government to withdraw troops as soon as possible. Moreover, in November 1931, the Chinese delegation submitted to the Council successive reports from Dr. Frederick A. Cleveland, the Associate Director of the Salt Inspectorate. According to these reports, the Japanese military authorities were at that time forcibly seizing salt taxes throughout Manchuria, while according to Japanese official documents, the Japanese military authorities were transferring the surplus funds from the Chinese salt tax organs to another Chinese organ (the Local Maintenance Committee), which could not be said to be improper.

(5) Council Meetings and the Organization of the Commission of Inquiry in November and December 1931: At this time, the Council was meeting in Paris on November 16. On November 21, the Japanese side proposed sending a Commission of Inquiry to the Far East to investigate, stating that "the Japanese government's sincere desire to withdraw troops to the South Manchuria Railway zone as soon as possible in accordance with the resolution of September 30 will not be changed by the establishment and dispatch of this Commission of Inquiry." After consideration, the Council adopted the following resolution on December 10:

(1) The Council reaffirms the resolution unanimously adopted on September 30, which both China and Japan have declared themselves solemnly bound by. Therefore, the Council requests the Chinese and Japanese governments to take the necessary steps to implement this resolution, so that the Japanese army may withdraw to the railway zone as soon as possible in accordance with the conditions set forth in the resolution. (2) The Council believes that the situation has become more serious since the meeting on November 24 and is aware that both parties have undertaken to take the necessary measures to prevent further escalation of the situation and to avoid any action that would lead to war and loss of life again. (3) The Council requests both parties to continue to notify the Council of the development of the situation at any time. (4) The Council requests other member states to provide the Council with information obtained locally by their representatives at any time. (5) In view of the special circumstances of this case, and desiring to assist the two governments in seeking a final and fundamental resolution of the various issues between the two governments, the Council, without prejudice to the implementation of the above-mentioned measures, decides to send a commission, composed of five members, to study on the spot any circumstances affecting international relations that may disrupt the peace between China and Japan or the understanding on which peace is maintained, and to report to the Council. The Chinese and Japanese governments may each send a member to assist the commission. The two governments should provide the commission with all facilities so that the commission may have access to any information it needs. If the two parties begin any negotiations, such negotiations are not within the scope of the commission's duties, and the commission has no right to interfere in any party's military measures. The appointment of the commission and its consideration shall not in any way hinder the Japanese government's guarantee in the resolution of September 30 for the withdrawal of the Japanese army to the railway zone. (6) Between now and the next regular meeting on January 25, the Council will continue to deal with this issue and requests the President to pay attention to this issue and to reconvene a meeting if necessary. (The President of the Council, the French representative Briand, when submitting this resolution, solemnly declared that the Council attaches extreme importance to the resolution of September 30 and its own belief that the two governments will fully fulfill the commitments of the resolution, and that it is necessary and urgent for both parties to avoid any action that would further lead to war or escalate the situation. The U.S. government expressed its pleasure at the passage of the resolution, as there had been definite progress.)

(6) Japanese Army Attacks Jinzhou: The Destruction of the Remaining Chinese Administrative Authority in the South Manchuria Area. When the Council was engaged in drafting the above-mentioned resolution, both China and Japan requested the Council to pay attention to the danger of military action extending to southwestern Manchuria. Consequently, there was an effort to establish a neutral zone between the Japanese army and Zhang Xueliang's troops in Jinzhou. However, this effort unfortunately failed. When the Japanese representative accepted Section 2 of the resolution, he stated that it should be understood that the purpose of the section was not to prevent the Japanese army from taking actions necessary to directly protect the lives and property of Japanese residents from the trampling of bandits or unlawful elements in various parts of Manchuria. This action was an exceptional measure based on the special circumstances in the Northeast. Once the normal state of affairs was restored in the area in the future, the necessity of this measure would naturally disappear. On December 23, the Japanese army began to advance towards Jinzhou and occupied it on January 3, 1932. The Japanese army then advanced to the Great Wall and made contact with the Japanese army stationed at Shanhaiguan, south of the Great Wall. The result of this military action was the complete destruction of the Chinese administrative authority in the South Manchuria area.

(7) Hostilities in Shanghai: The Origin of Hostilities. After January 1932, the situation in areas outside Manchuria became increasingly precarious, as was the case in Shanghai. Regarding the Shanghai Incident, the Consular Corps Committee, which was organized in Shanghai in early February, collected a total of four reports describing the course of the incident from the beginning to March 5. The subsequent events are recorded in the report of the Commission of Inquiry. The Commission of Inquiry, as explained above, was established in January 1932 and arrived in Shanghai on March 14. Prior to this, there had been serious anti-Chinese riots in Korea. As stated in the report of the Commission of Inquiry, these riots triggered the boycott of Japanese goods in Shanghai and other ports in China after June 1931. The Japanese army's occupation of Manchuria intensified the boycott. In some cases, the Chinese government and official organizations actively assisted, causing significant losses to Japanese businesses. The tense feelings between the people of the two countries became increasingly acute, and serious incidents soon occurred. As a result, Japanese residents in Shanghai requested their government to send troops and warships to stop the anti-Japanese movement. Subsequently, the Japanese Consul-General presented five conditions to the Mayor of Shanghai, China. On January 21, the Mayor of Shanghai stated that it was difficult to comply with two of the conditions (i.e., fully suppressing the anti-Japanese movement and dissolving all anti-Japanese organizations that provoked ill feelings and incited anti-Japanese riots). On the same day, the Japanese naval commander announced that if the Chinese Mayor's reply was unsatisfactory, he would take necessary steps to protect Japanese rights and interests. On January 24, additional Japanese naval troops arrived in Shanghai. Rumors spread that Chinese troops stationed in Zhabei District, in the Chinese-controlled area, were also increasing their numbers. On January 27, the Japanese Consul-General demanded that the Chinese side provide a satisfactory reply to the conditions by 6:00 a.m. the next morning. The Mayor of Shanghai expressed his intention to the representatives of various countries to make as many concessions as possible to avoid conflict. On the evening of January 27 to 28, the Anti-Japanese Association was suspended, and other anti-Japanese organizations were closed down by the Chinese police. On the morning of January 28, the Japanese naval commander notified the commanders of the armed forces of various countries that if the Chinese side did not provide a satisfactory reply, he would take action the next morning. The Shanghai Municipal Council held a meeting and decided to declare martial law from 4:00 p.m. that afternoon. At 4:00 p.m., the Japanese Consul-General notified the Consular Corps that he had received a Chinese reply accepting all Japanese conditions, which could be considered completely satisfactory, and that no action would be taken for the time being. At the same time, the Defense Committee of the Public Concession, in order to adapt to the emergency situation at the time, divided the Concession into areas and assigned the armed forces of various countries to be responsible for defense. The Japanese defense zone designated by the Defense Committee not only included a part of the Concession but also extended beyond the Concession, westward to the Songhu Railway. The Japanese naval headquarters was located at the northernmost end of this extended area. The Beisichuan Road and Diswell Road, which belonged to the Municipal Council, usually had Japanese naval marines stationed there. At 11:00 p.m., the Japanese naval commander declared that, in view of the current emergency situation, the Imperial Navy was extremely concerned about the situation in Zhabei, where a large number of Japanese residents lived, and had decided to send troops to the area, hoping that the Chinese troops stationed in Zhabei would quickly withdraw to the west of the railway. An hour later, Japanese marines and armed civilians advanced towards the railway. The last team attempted to enter the station through the gate of Henan Road, which entered the Concession and the defense zone, but was stopped by the Shanghai Volunteer Corps stationed in the area. The Shanghai Volunteer Corps had strict orders, the principle of which was that the duty of the defending troops was limited to defense and not to attack. According to the defense plan, the Japanese troops sent to the Zhabei section came into contact with the Chinese troops. According to the first report of the Consular Corps Committee, the Chinese troops, even if they were willing to withdraw, were prevented from doing so by time.

(8) Hostilities in Shanghai: Council Discussions Based on Article 10 of the Covenant; First Discussion of the Assembly Based on Article 15; The End of Hostilities in Shanghai. The Shanghai War thus began. The Council, which was meeting in Geneva at the time, and the countries with special interests in Shanghai, repeatedly worked to stop it. After the above-mentioned serious incident occurred, China requested on January 29 that the dispute be handled in accordance with Articles 10 and 15. On February 16, the member states of the Council, except for China and Japan, presented an urgent request to the Japanese government to pay attention to Article 10 of the Covenant. According to the meaning of the article, "Any disregard of the provisions of the article, damage to the territorial integrity of a member state of the League of Nations, and alteration of its political independence shall not be recognized as valid by the member states of the League." On February 19, the Council, at the request of China, submitted the dispute to the Assembly. The Assembly convened a meeting on March 3. Before the Assembly met, the Council made a final effort to stop the war and proposed on February 29 to organize a round-table conference in Shanghai, but its holding would have to wait until measures to stop the hostilities had been agreed upon locally. The Council's proposal was not implemented because the war continued. On March 3, after hearing the statements of the representatives of both sides, the Assembly passed the following resolution on March 4: "The Assembly, in restating the proposal decided by the Council on February 29, and declaring that it does not prejudice other principal methods contained in the proposal, (1) requests the Chinese and Japanese governments to immediately take the necessary measures to make the cease-fire orders issued by the military authorities of both sides effective; (2) requests the major powers with special interests in Shanghai to report to the Assembly on the state of implementation of the aforementioned measures; (3) advises the Chinese and Japanese representatives to begin discussing and establishing measures with the assistance of the above-mentioned major powers, military and civilian officials. These measures must ensure the cessation of hostilities and stipulate the withdrawal of the Japanese army. Please report the progress of the consultations to the Assembly at any time." (On March 5, the U.S. government indicated that it had instructed its military officials in Shanghai to fully cooperate.) The consultations proposed by various parties began on March 14 in Shanghai. The Committee of Nineteen, organized by the Assembly, twice mediated at the request of China to eliminate various difficulties and finally signed a cease-fire agreement in Shanghai on May 5. On the 6th of the same month, the Japanese army began to withdraw. By May 31, all divisions sent by Japan to Shanghai had re-embarked. Among these divisions, only the 14th Division was reassigned to Manchuria. On July 1, the Assembly received a report stating that only a very small number of Japanese marines were temporarily stationed in a few places adjacent to the Concession and the roads built beyond the boundary, in accordance with the agreement of May 5. Subsequently, these troops were also withdrawn. The Chinese side believes that Japan's intervention in Shanghai caused 24,000 deaths, injuries, and disappearances of Chinese soldiers and civilians, and the material losses are estimated to be worth more than 1.5 billion yuan.

(9) Progress of Japanese Occupation in Manchuria: The Implementation of Administrative Organization; The Constitution of "Manchukuo." While the Shanghai Incident was developing, the situation in Manchuria was also progressing. On February 5, Harbin was occupied by Japanese troops. In the following months, Japanese troops continued to carry out military operations against the remnants of the Chinese army, as well as "volunteer armies," bandits, and various other "informal troops." Small-scale battles spread over a large part of Manchuria. At the same time, administrative reorganization was also taking place. Its initial stages have been described above. On February 17, 1932, a Supreme Administrative Council was established for the entire Manchuria. On February 18, the Council issued a declaration of independence. On February 19, the Japanese representative explained at the Council meeting in Geneva that the meaning of "independence" in Manchuria was the same as the meaning of "autonomy." Japan viewed the success of this independence with a favorable attitude. On March 9, the various local administrative organs were merged into an independent state called "Manchukuo." The position of head of state was inherited by Puyi, the former Xuantong Emperor of the Qing Dynasty. The Chinese government had claimed on November 17, 1931, that the deposed emperor had been abducted by the Japanese from the Japanese Concession in Tianjin and escorted to Shenyang, with the aim of establishing a puppet government with the deposed emperor as emperor. The Chinese government has repeatedly denounced the establishment of this so-called state as illegal, and all the creations and maintenance of this so-called state from its inception and in its subsequent development have been directed and assisted by the Japanese army stationed in Manchuria. (10) Discussions of the Assembly: The Resolution of March 11 Regarding the Decision on the Deadline for Preparing the Report in Accordance with Article 15 of the Covenant. At the same time, the Assembly continued to study the dispute in Geneva. After detailed discussions on March 11, 1932, it passed the following resolution: Section 1, the Assembly, in view of the provisions contained in the Covenant, is fully applicable to this dispute, especially regarding (1) the principle of strict respect for treaties, (2) the promise of member states of the League to respect and maintain the territorial integrity and existing political independence of all members of the League to defend against external aggression, (3) the obligation to resolve all disputes with each other by adopting the principles established in the declaration of December 10, 1931, by Briand, the President of the Council. The twelve members of the Council had reiterated this declaration of principles in their application to the Japanese government on February 16, 1932, declaring that any disregard of the provisions of Article 10 of the Covenant, trampling on the territorial integrity of a member state of the League, and altering its political independence should not be recognized as valid by member states of the League. In view of the above provisions, the principles of international relations of member states of the League and the peaceful resolution of all disputes are fully in line with the Pact of Paris, which is the cornerstone of the world peace mechanism, and Article 2 of which stipulates, "The contracting parties agree that all disputes between them, regardless of their nature or origin, shall be resolved only by peaceful means." Before taking the final steps to resolve the dispute it has accepted, the Assembly declares that the above-mentioned principles and provisions have a binding nature that must be observed and declares that any position, treaty, or agreement obtained by methods that violate the Covenant of the League and the Pact of Paris shall not be recognized by members of the League. Section 2 The Assembly solemnly declares that any resolution of the Sino-Japanese dispute obtained by the use of force by any party is contrary to the spirit of the Covenant. Recalling the resolutions passed by the Council with the consent of both parties on September 20 and December 10, 1931, and recalling the resolution passed by the Assembly itself on March 4, 1932, with the consent of both parties, regarding the effective cessation of hostilities and the withdrawal of the Japanese army, the Assembly is aware that countries with special interests in the Shanghai Concession are fully prepared to assist in this endeavor and requests that these countries fully cooperate to maintain order in the area of withdrawal when necessary. Section 3 The Assembly, in view of the request of the Chinese government on January 29 to apply the procedures of Article 15 of the Covenant of the League to this dispute, in view of the request of the Chinese government on February 12 to submit this dispute to the Assembly in accordance with Section 9 of Article 15 of the Covenant, and in view of the decision of the Council on February 19, considering that the Assembly, having accepted the entirety of the dispute referred to in the request of the Chinese government, should bear the obligation to apply the mediation procedures stipulated in Section 3 of Article 15 of the Covenant and, if necessary, should bear the obligation to apply the procedures of "explanation and recommendation" stipulated in Section 4 of the same article, decides to organize a committee of nineteen members, with the President of the Assembly as the chairman of the committee, together with the members of the Council other than the parties concerned, and representatives of other member states elected by secret ballot. The committee shall perform its duties on behalf of the Assembly and shall be subject to the supervision of the Assembly. It shall (1) promptly report on the cessation of hostilities and the conclusion of agreements in accordance with the Assembly's resolution of March 4, 1923, to effectively stop the war in Shanghai and stipulate the withdrawal of the Japanese army; (2) pay attention to the implementation of the resolutions passed by the Council on September 30 and December 10, 1931; (3) with the consent of both parties and in accordance with the provisions of Section 3 of Article 15 of the Covenant, undertake preparations for resolving the dispute and draft a statement to be submitted to the Assembly; (4) if necessary, propose to the Assembly to submit to the International Court of Justice a request for the expression of opinions; (5) if necessary, undertake preparations for a draft report as stipulated in Section 4 of Article 15; (6) recommend all urgent measures that appear necessary; (7) submit the first report to the Assembly at the earliest possible time, no later than May 1, 1932. The Assembly requests the Council to forward all documents or incidental opinions that it deems should be forwarded to the Assembly to the committee. The Assembly is not adjourned, and the Chairman may convene it when he deems it necessary. On March 12, the U.S. government declared that the measures of the League of Nations Assembly were sufficient to make the principles of security and justice on which the Pact of Non-Aggression and the Covenant of the League of Nations were based become international law. The British government was particularly pleased that the countries of the world were now adopting a united policy of not recognizing the validity of the results of violations of these treaties, which was a special contribution to international law and a solid foundation for peacebuilding. On May 1, 1932, the League of Nations Assembly received a report that the report of the Commission of Inquiry could not be drafted and completed before September. With the consent of both parties, the Assembly decided to extend the six-month period for preparing the report stipulated by the Covenant of the League of Nations to the extent that was truly necessary. When the President of the League of Nations Assembly sent a letter to the Chinese and Japanese representatives on June 24 proposing to extend the deadline stipulated in the Covenant, he stated, "It is my duty to offer a further word. I firmly believe that both parties will abide by their lies in the Council not to expand the situation. This promise has been expressly stated in the resolutions of September 30 and October 10 (1931), and these resolutions still have full executive force. These resolutions will continue to have full force during the extended period after the six-month deadline expires. Your representative will agree with me on this point. What is also brought to your attention is that the resolution passed by the League of Nations Assembly on March 11 has reiterated these two resolutions." After the League of Nations Assembly passed the extension of the deadline, the President of the League of Nations Assembly stated in his letter, "Since this is the case, the decision adopted by the Assembly authorizes me to declare that the parties concerned must not take any action that would jeopardize the success of the work of the Commission of Inquiry or the efforts of the League of Nations to promote a solution."

(11) The Organization of Manchukuo. The procedures for organizing Manchukuo continued while Japan recognized Manchukuo. The government established a central bank and took over the salt tax administration, declaring its willingness to continue to pay the balance of the foreign debt required, which was guaranteed by salt tax revenue. It made the same declaration on customs administration, regarding debts and reparations guaranteed by customs duties, and on postal administration. The creation of Manchukuo's army was aided by Japanese officials who were hired as advisors. In its notification of April 8, 1923, the Japanese government declared that it was currently providing assistance to the Manchukuo army in a friendly spirit to meet its needs for maintaining security and restoring order. According to the Japanese government's opinion in its document of November 18, 1932, the most important bandits in the eastern provinces could be eliminated by Japanese troops in the second and third years. The relationship between Japan and the new organization was also confirmed after General Muto was sent to Changchun, the capital of "

Fundraising for Vagrant Vocational Institute 86

Various agencies and organizations under the jurisdiction of Songjiang County, in order to relieve unemployed vagrants and seek local economic benefits, established the Vagrant Vocational Institute, which was established on January 1st. Director Du Lianhua and Director Zhong Changfeng are managing the affairs. The scale is roughly in place, but at the beginning of the establishment, there is a huge shortage of funds. It is planned to raise donations from all walks of life to subsidize the operation. The fundraising announcement roughly states: Unemployed vagrants in the area endanger social security, which is not a trivial matter. Various groups in our county, in view of this, plan to find ways to provide relief in order to seek social stability. Therefore, preparations were made on January 1st of the 22nd year, and it was officially established to teach knowledge and skills, so that the vagrants will have the hope of self-reliance in the future. However, our institute is just starting, and everything is waiting to be done. The economy is particularly difficult, and the future is long and difficult to maintain. We would like to call on all great philanthropists to donate generously and enthusiastically support us, so that we can gather strength and work together to accomplish this task.

Renaming of Xicheng Road in Wuxi 87

The Jiangsu Provincial Department of Construction, in order to develop transportation in this province,

Specially formulated the Jiangsu Transportation Three-Year Plan. The names of the original highways in this province should be the same as those determined by the highway conference of the seven provinces of Jiangsu, Zhejiang, Anhui, Jiangxi, Hunan, Hubei, and Henan. Yesterday, a special order was issued to various counties to change to the new names. The Xicheng Road in Wuxi is the highway from Wuxi to Jiangyin. It has now been renamed from Jiangyin to Wuxi as the Chengxi Section, and from Wuxi to Mudu, Suzhou as the Xisu Section.

Junhe Association Stipulates the Commencement Date of Each River Project 88

The County Dredging River Commission held its fourth standing committee meeting at the Construction Bureau yesterday (the 17th) at 2 pm. Chairman Xiao Zicai and recorder Zhang Mingou discussed the following items: 1. Commissioner Xiao Zicai proposed how to stipulate the commencement date of each river. The resolution was that March 5th is the commencement date for the first phase, and it must be completed before March 24th. March 25th is the commencement date for the second phase. All preparatory matters must be completed before the commencement date. 2. Commissioner Huang Decun proposed how to stipulate the commencement date of each river in phases. It was resolved that Furong Pond (ie, Anzhen River), Nanxing Pond, Shuang River, and Mudu be included in the first phase, and Da River, Zhihu Port, and Ma Port be included in the second phase. The meeting adjourned after the discussion.

Expansion of Business at the Farmers Bank 89

Wuyi Farmers Bank, managed by Jiang Xichang, has been operating for nearly four years since its establishment in the 18th year. The business is still considered to be developing. Last year's statistics showed that loans amounted to 460,000 yuan, warehouse loans amounted to more than 123,000 yuan, and the total annual profit was 18,000 yuan. Staff member Mr. Yang has been sent to preside over the office. Locals Fang Zhiyi and others recently felt that transportation in Jiangbei was inconvenient, so they raised funds to establish a long-distance bus company between Yangzhou and Taizhou. According to the county road from Yangzhou to Taizhou, the bus passes through Xianzhen, Yiling, and Baituohe. Jiashan

Dayun Temple Town Pillaged by Bandits, Nine People Kidnapped 92

Dayun Temple Town, located in the southern countryside of the county and adjacent to Jiaxing County, was suddenly attacked by more than a hundred bandits at 3 pm on the 16th. First, four or five bandits in blue uniforms mixed into the security team and the police station, robbing six quick guns from the security team and three quick guns from the police station. The remaining bandits then robbed house by house, causing losses of about several thousand yuan. Later, they kidnapped nine people, including the son of Yao Shengquan, the head of Dayun Temple Town, and the son of Shen Qixiang, who works at the meat shop. When the district regiment headquarters heard the news, they went to investigate, but the bandits had already fled far away.

Ningbo

Zhenhai County Government Holds Emergency Meeting 93

The Zhenhai Temple Fair, with more than 3,000 people, destroyed the County Public Security Bureau on the evening of the 14th. The County Government held an emergency meeting on the 15th. The results of the discussion were: 1. A public announcement was issued jointly by the general headquarters, the water police bureau, and the county government, strictly prohibiting the public from holding temple fairs and gatherings. 2. The city and suburbs will be under martial law. The military and police agencies originally responsible will intensify their defenses. Any temple fairs and gatherings that have not been approved by the government will be stopped and disbanded by the responsible teams in each defense zone. 3. The expenses for the damage to the police station and the repair of the houses will be advanced by the county government for 500 yuan. The county government will also convene local gentry to discuss the aftermath. In addition, the ringleaders of the riot have been arrested one after another: Li Xinfa, Gu Xinbao, Chen Asi, Zhang Heqing, Liu Guiqing, Weng Ayin, Peng Jinyin, and others. They are temporarily detained by the county government.

Mutinous Soldiers of Liu's Troops Rampage Daishan Island 94

The other day, seventy to eighty rioters rampaged around Daishan Island. It is said that these rioters were indeed part of Liu Zhennian's troops who had previously mutinied and fled in Wenzhou. They suddenly mutinied in Taishun County, Wenzhou the month before, looting for three days and nights, and then fled to eastern Fujian and Zhejiang, carrying out large-scale looting. Among them, seventy to eighty people carrying weapons seized four small sailing boats on the 14th and fled to Gaoting Town, Daishan, which belongs to Dinghai. The local security team came forward to stop them, and several conflicts broke out. The residents of Gaoting were panicked. Later, after the local people pleaded earnestly and provided rations, they boarded the Da Xing steamer and left Gaoting for Dinghai. After arriving in Dinghai, they landed at Zhushantou, ten miles away from Dinghai, and began to loot. After learning that mutinous soldiers had been found in Daishan, the Dinghai County Government telegraphed the Yong Defense Headquarters and the Zhenhai Water Police Bureau for help. After receiving the telegram, the Defense Headquarters sent thirty to forty soldiers on the Zhenbei Gao steamer to Yue Mountain for defense. The Water Police Bureau has also sent the Flying Eagle patrol boat to assist. It is also reported that the mutinous soldiers in Daishan are fully equipped with guns and ammunition, and they also have machine gunners and grenade launchers.

Yang Jianping, the security brigade adjutant, previously led a team to capture the bandit Ma Saizi in Lao Jia Shan. He also captured kidnappers Shen Changyou and Zhu Leping, as well as the kidnapped victim Huang Zhangshi, in Ao Sun Village under Da Hong Shan. After interrogation, they all confessed to being bandits. In addition to ordering the three kidnapped victims to be claimed by their families, County Chief Li executed the three bandits on the 13th.

Full Text of the League of Nations Report Draft

(Nanjing) The full text of the report drafted by the League of Nations Committee of Nineteen was translated and released by the Ministry of Foreign Affairs on the evening of the 18th. The original text is as follows:

Draft Report of the League of Nations Assembly 96

The Assembly's efforts in accordance with Article 15, paragraph 3 of the Covenant, to resolve the dispute submitted to the Assembly for discussion in accordance with paragraph 9 of that Article, having unfortunately failed, hereby adopts the following report in accordance with the provisions of paragraph 4 of the same Article, in order to record the facts of the dispute and the recommendations considered fair and appropriate.

Part 1: The Incident in the Far East 97

Adoption of the First Eight Chapters of the League of Nations Inquiry Report and the Plan of This Report: The fundamental causes of the Sino-Japanese dispute are very complex. The investigation team dispatched by the Executive Yuan to conduct on-site research stated that "the various issues contained in this dispute are not as simple as they are often said to be. This case is extremely complex. Only those who have a deep knowledge of all the facts and their historical background can express practical opinions on this case." The first eight chapters of the investigation team's report contain a fair and detailed account of the historical background of the Sino-Japanese dispute and the important facts related to Manchuria. The report has been printed separately. It is impossible and unnecessary to summarize or repeat it here. After studying the written opinions submitted by the Chinese and Japanese governments, the Assembly adopted the opinions in the first eight chapters of the investigation team's report as part of this report. However, in order to make the statement in the investigation team's report complete, the various measures taken by the Executive Yuan and the Assembly on various aspects of this dispute, as well as certain facts not mentioned in the investigation team's report, are described, especially the origin of the Battle of Shanghai in early 1932. Regarding these events, the Assembly adopted the report sent by the consular investigation team of various countries to the Assembly (this report has been printed separately) as part of this report. It is also necessary to restate the details of the Manchurian incidents since the beginning of September 1932, because the investigation team's report did not trace back to that date. A brief historical account of the development of this dispute will be contained in Chapter 2 of this report, and the factual account in the investigation team's report must be consulted at the same time. Chapter 3 states the important features of this dispute and the conclusions drawn by the Assembly based on the main facts. Chapter 4 contains the recommendations that the Assembly considers fair and appropriate for this case.

Sun Ke's Views on the Report 98

(If the powers cannot impose economic sanctions on Japan, this report is also just a piece of paper)

Sun Ke, President of the Legislative Yuan, arrived in Shanghai from Nanjing the night before last. At 10 am yesterday, he met with reporters from various newspapers at his residence on Columbia Road and expressed his opinions on the report of the Committee of Nineteen and the abolition of special laws. The details are recorded as follows:

Criticizing the Report: According to Mr. Sun, the report of the Committee of Nineteen of the League of Nations is very fair in determining that Japan should be responsible for the September 18th Incident, not recognizing the puppet organization, and inviting the United States and Russia to participate. It can be said to be satisfactory. However, the main key lies in whether the League of Nations can implement the report. In other words, it can decide not to sanction countries that violate the covenant. According to news from Geneva, the powers are still hesitant to impose economic sanctions on Japan. If the League of Nations cannot use economic sanctions to sanction Japan, which violates the covenant, then this report is also just a piece of paper.

Abolishing Special Laws: At present, in addition to the Criminal Law, our country still has various special laws such as the Law on Punishing Crimes Endangering the Republic of China in Emergency. It is very inconvenient to implement them at the same time. At present, the Legislative Yuan is working on revising the Criminal Law, and plans to include the crimes and penalties of various special laws in it, so that when the revised Criminal Law comes into effect, various special laws can be abolished.

Wu and Sun Talk About the National Political Consultative Council

The Organizational Regulations Have Been Submitted to the Central Political Committee, and the Task of the Regulations Drafting Committee Has Ended 99

Regarding the organization of the National Political Consultative Council, the Central Political Committee previously appointed Wu Chaochu, Dai Jitao, and others to be responsible for drafting it, so that after it is passed, it can be handed over to the Legislative Yuan to formulate detailed regulations. Yesterday, reporters from the Central News Agency interviewed Mr. Wu Chaochu and Mr. Sun Zhesheng separately to inquire about the progress of this matter. The two gentlemen were received and replied as follows:

Wu Chaochu said: According to Mr. Wu Chaochu, the drafting committee of the organizational regulations of the National Political Consultative Council had gathered several times to discuss various issues related to the organization of the National Political Consultative Council. At present, the opinions of the members on various issues have become consistent and have been passed separately. However, the highest decision-making power on this issue belongs to the Central Political Conference. Therefore, the members have submitted the drafted organizational regulations to the Central Political Conference so that the Political Conference can make the final revisions and decisions, and then hand them over to the Legislative Yuan to formulate detailed regulations. As for the task of the drafting committee of the organizational regulations of the National Political Consultative Council, it has now ended.

Sun Zhesheng said: According to Mr. Sun Zhesheng, the organizational regulations of the National Political Consultative Council will probably be submitted to the Central Political Conference for discussion next week. After they are passed, they will be handed over to the Legislative Yuan to formulate detailed regulations. As for the candidates for the National Political Consultative Council, most of them will be elected and a few will be appointed. The election will be distributed according to the population of each province and city, with a maximum of six people per province and city. The total number is about one hundred people.
